<p><b>Venice</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small>English</small> <span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">VEN</span></span></b>-iss</i></a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Venezia</i></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Italian" title="Help:IPA for Italian">[veˈnɛttsja]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<a href="/wiki/File:It-Venezia.ogg" title="File:It-Venezia.ogg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" width="13" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/It-Venezia.ogg" class="internal" title="It-Venezia.ogg">listen</a>)</small>; <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian</a>: <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec"><i>Venesia</i></span>) is a city in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">Veneto</a> <a href="/wiki/Italian_regions" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian regions">region</a>. It is situated across a group of 117 small islands that are separated by canals and linked by bridges.<sup id="cite_ref-UNESCO_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-1">[1]</a></sup> These are located in the shallow <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Lagoon" title="Venetian Lagoon">Venetian Lagoon</a> which stretches along the shoreline, between the mouths of the <a href="/wiki/Po_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Po River">Po</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Piave_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Piave River">Piave</a> Rivers. Parts of Venice are renowned for the beauty of their settings, their <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, and artwork.<sup id="cite_ref-UNESCO_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-1">[1]</a></sup> The lagoon and a part of the city are listed as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UNESCO_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, 270,098 people resided in Venice's <i><a href="/wiki/Comune" title="Comune">comune</a></i> (the population estimate of 272,000 inhabitants includes the population of the whole Comune of Venezia; of whom around 60,000<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> live in the historic city of Venice (<i>Centro storico</i>); 176,000 in <i>Terraferma</i> (the mainland), mostly in the large <i><a href="/wiki/Frazione" title="Frazione">frazioni</a></i> (roughly equivalent to "parishes" or "<a href="/wiki/Ward_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ward (country subdivision)">wards</a>" in other countries) of <a href="/wiki/Mestre" title="Mestre">Mestre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marghera" title="Marghera">Marghera</a>; and 31,000 on other islands in the lagoon). Together with <a href="/wiki/Padua,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Padua, Italy">Padua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treviso,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Treviso, Italy">Treviso</a>, the city is included in the <a href="/wiki/Padua-Treviso-Venice_Metropolitan_Area" title="Padua-Treviso-Venice Metropolitan Area">Padua-Treviso-Venice Metropolitan Area</a> (PATREVE), with a total population of 2,600,000. PATREVE is only a statistical metropolitan area without any degree of autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name is derived from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Veneti" title="Adriatic Veneti">Veneti</a> people who inhabited the region by the 10th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-urlOnline_Etymology_Dictionary_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlOnline_Etymology_Dictionary-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The city was historically the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>. Venice has been known as the "La Dominante," "Serenissima," "Queen of the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a>," "City of Water," "City of Masks," "City of Bridges," "The Floating City," and "City of Canals."</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> was a major financial and maritime power during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Staging_area" title="Staging area">staging area</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lepanto_(1571)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Lepanto (1571)">Battle of Lepanto</a>, as well as a very important center of commerce (especially silk, grain, and <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice</a>) and art in the 13th century up to the end of the 17th century. The City State of Venice is considered to have been the first real international financial center which gradually emerged from the 9th century to its peak in the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> This made Venice a wealthy city throughout most of its history.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> It is also known for its several important artistic movements, especially the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Renaissance period</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a>, the Republic was annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a>, until it became part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a> in 1866, following a referendum held as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Italian_War_of_Independence" title="Third Italian War of Independence">Third Italian War of Independence</a>. Venice has played an important role in the history of symphonic and operatic music, and it is the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Antonio Vivaldi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of the city, deriving from Latin forms <i>Venetia</i> and <i>Venetiae</i>, is most likely taken from "Venetia et Histria", the Roman name of <i><a href="/wiki/Triveneto" title="Triveneto">Regio X</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Roman Italy</a>, but applied to the coastal part of the region that remained under <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Roman Empire</a> outside of Gothic, Lombard, and Frankish control. The name <i>Venetia</i>, however, derives from the Roman name for the people known as the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Veneti" title="Adriatic Veneti">Veneti</a>, and called by the Greeks <i>Eneti</i> (Ἐνετοί). The meaning of the word is uncertain, although there are other Indo-European tribes with similar-sounding names, such as the <a href="/wiki/Veneti_(Gaul)" title="Veneti (Gaul)">Celtic Veneti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vistula_Veneti" title="Vistula Veneti">Baltic Veneti</a>, and the Slavic <a href="/wiki/Wends" title="Wends">Wends</a>. Linguists suggest that the name is based on an <a href="/wiki/Protoindoeuropean" class="mw-redirect" title="Protoindoeuropean">Indo-European</a> root *<i>wen</i> ("love"), so that *<i>wenetoi</i> would mean "beloved", "lovable", or "friendly". A connection with the Latin word <i>venetus</i>, meaning the color 'sea-blue', is also possible. Supposed connections of <i>Venetia</i> with the Latin verb <i>venire</i> (to come), such as <a href="/wiki/Marino_Sanuto_the_Younger" title="Marino Sanuto the Younger">Marin Sanudo</a>'s <i>veni etiam</i> ("Yet, I have come!"), the supposed cry of the first refugees to the Venetian lagoon from the mainland, or even with <i>venia</i> ("forgiveness") <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">are fanciful</a>. The alternative obsolete form is <i>Vinegia</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Italian" title="Help:IPA for Italian">[viˈnɛːdʒa]</a></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian</a>: <i>Venèxia</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Venetian" title="Help:IPA for Venetian">[veˈnɛzja]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Venetiae</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a>: <span lang="sl" xml:lang="sl"><i>Benetke</i></span>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Venice" title="History of the Republic of Venice">History of the Republic of Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Venice" title="Timeline of Venice">Timeline of Venice</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although no surviving historical records deal directly with the founding of Venice,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> tradition and the available evidence have led several historians to agree that the original population of Venice consisted of refugees from Roman cities near Venice such as <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquileia" title="Aquileia">Aquileia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treviso" title="Treviso">Treviso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altinum" title="Altinum">Altino</a> and Concordia (modern <a href="/wiki/Portogruaro" title="Portogruaro">Portogruaro</a>) and from the undefended countryside, who were fleeing successive waves of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Hun</a> invasions.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Some late Roman sources reveal the existence of fishermen on the islands in the original marshy lagoons. They were referred to as <i>incolae lacunae</i> ("lagoon dwellers"). The traditional founding is identified with the dedication of the first church, that of <a href="/wiki/San_Giacomo_di_Rialto" title="San Giacomo di Rialto">San Giacomo</a> on the islet of <a href="/wiki/Rialto" title="Rialto">Rialto</a> (Rivoalto, "High Shore") — said to have taken place at the stroke of noon on 25 March 421 (the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Annunciation" title="Feast of the Annunciation">Feast of the Annunciation</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RingSalkin1996_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RingSalkin1996-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning as early as AD 166 to 168, the <a href="/wiki/Quadi" title="Quadi">Quadi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcomanni" title="Marcomanni">Marcomanni</a> destroyed the main center in the area, the current <a href="/wiki/Oderzo" title="Oderzo">Oderzo</a>. The Roman defences were again overthrown in the early 5th century by the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a> and, some 50 years later, by the Huns led by <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a>. The last and most enduring immigration into the north of the Italian peninsula, that of the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> in 568, left the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> a small strip of coast in the current Veneto, including Venice. The Roman/Byzantine territory was organized as the <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Ravenna" title="Exarchate of Ravenna">Exarchate of Ravenna</a>, administered from that ancient port and overseen by a viceroy (the <a href="/wiki/Exarch" title="Exarch">Exarch</a>) appointed by the Emperor in Constantinople, but Ravenna and Venice were connected only by sea routes; and with the Venetians' isolated position came increasing autonomy. New ports were built, including those at Malamocco and <a href="/wiki/Torcello" title="Torcello">Torcello</a> in the Venetian lagoon. The <i>tribuni maiores</i>, the earliest central standing governing committee of the islands in the Lagoon, dated from c. 568.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The traditional first <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">doge of Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Lucio_Anafesto" title="Paolo Lucio Anafesto">Paolo Lucio Anafesto</a>, was actually <a href="/wiki/Paul_(exarch)" title="Paul (exarch)">Exarch Paul</a>, and his successor, <a href="/wiki/Marcello_Tegalliano" title="Marcello Tegalliano">Marcello Tegalliano</a>, was Paul's <i><a href="/wiki/Magister_militum" title="Magister militum">magister militum</a></i> (General: literally, "Master of Soldiers"). In 726 the soldiers and citizens of the Exarchate rose in a rebellion over the <a href="/wiki/Iconoclastic_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Iconoclastic controversy">iconoclastic controversy</a> at the urging of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_II" title="Pope Gregory II">Pope Gregory II</a>. The Exarch was murdered and many officials put to flight in the chaos. At about this time, the people of the lagoon elected their own leader for the first time, although the relationship of this to the uprisings is not clear. <a href="/wiki/Orso_Ipato" title="Orso Ipato">Ursus</a> was the first of 117 "<a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">doges</a>" (<i>doge</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian dialect</a> development of the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Dux" title="Dux">dux</a></i> ("leader"); the corresponding word in English is <i><a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">duke</a></i>, in standard Italian <i><a href="/wiki/Duce" title="Duce">duce</a></i>.) Whatever his original views, Ursus supported Emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a>'s successful military expedition to recover Ravenna, sending both men and ships. In recognition of this, Venice was "granted numerous privileges and concessions" and Ursus, who had personally taken the field, was confirmed by Leo as <i><a href="/wiki/Dux" title="Dux">dux</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and given the added title of <i><a href="/wiki/Hypatus" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypatus">hypatus</a></i> (Greek for "<a href="/wiki/Consul" title="Consul">Consul</a>".)<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 751 the Lombard King <a href="/wiki/Aistulf" title="Aistulf">Aistulf</a> conquered most of the Exarchate of Ravenna, leaving Venice a lonely and increasingly autonomous Byzantine outpost. During this period, the seat of the local Byzantine governor (the "duke/dux", later "doge"), was situated in <a href="/wiki/Malamocco" title="Malamocco">Malamocco</a>. Settlement on the islands in the lagoon probably increased with the Lombard conquest of other Byzantine territories, as refugees sought asylum there. In 775/6 the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">episcopal</a> seat of Olivolo (<a href="/wiki/San_Pietro_di_Castello" class="mw-redirect" title="San Pietro di Castello">San Pietro di Castello</a>; Helipolis<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) was created. During the reign of duke <a href="/wiki/Agnello_Particiaco" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnello Particiaco">Agnello Particiaco</a> (811–827) the ducal seat moved from Malamocco to the highly protected Rialto, the current location of Venice. The monastery of St Zachary and the first <a href="/wiki/Doge%27s_Palace,_Venice" class="mw-redirect" title="Doge's Palace, Venice">ducal palace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St._Mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of St. Mark">basilica of St. Mark</a>, as well as a walled defense (<i>civitatis murus</i>) between Olivolo and Rialto, were subsequently built here.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> sought to subdue the city to his own rule. He ordered the Pope to expel the Venetians from the <a href="/wiki/Pentapolis" title="Pentapolis">Pentapolis</a> along the Adriatic coast,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and Charlemagne's own son <a href="/wiki/Pepin_of_Italy" title="Pepin of Italy">Pepin of Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Lombards" class="mw-redirect" title="King of the Lombards">king of the Lombards</a> under the authority of his father, embarked on a siege of Venice itself. This, however, proved a costly failure. The siege lasted six months, with Pepin's army ravaged by the diseases of the local swamps and eventually forced to withdraw (810). A few months later, Pepin himself died, apparently as a result of a disease contracted there. In the aftermath, an agreement between <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> and the Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_I_Logothetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Nikephoros I Logothetes">Nicephorus</a> in 814 recognized Venice as Byzantine territory and granted the city trading rights along the Adriatic coast.</p>
<p>In 828 the new city's prestige increased with the acquisition of the claimed relics of <a href="/wiki/St_Mark_the_Evangelist" class="mw-redirect" title="St Mark the Evangelist">St Mark the Evangelist</a> from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, which were placed in the new basilica. (Winged lions, visible throughout Venice, symbolise St Mark.) The patriarchal seat also moved to Rialto. As the community continued to develop and as Byzantine power waned, its autonomy grew, leading to eventual independence.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion">Expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:P1060341.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/P1060341.jpg/220px-P1060341.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/P1060341.jpg/330px-P1060341.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/P1060341.jpg/440px-P1060341.jpg 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="1267" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:P1060341.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Piazza_San_Marco" title="Piazza San Marco">Piazza San Marco</a> in Venice, with <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Campanile" title="St Mark's Campanile">St&#160;Mark's&#160;Campanile</a> and Basilica in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Marco_horses.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/San_Marco_horses.jpg/170px-San_Marco_horses.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/San_Marco_horses.jpg/255px-San_Marco_horses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/San_Marco_horses.jpg/340px-San_Marco_horses.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Marco_horses.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
These <a href="/wiki/Horses_of_Saint_Mark" title="Horses of Saint Mark">Horses of Saint Mark</a> are a replica of the <i>Triumphal Quadriga</i> captured in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 1204 and carried to Venice as a trophy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the 9th to the 12th century, Venice developed into a <a href="/wiki/City_state" class="mw-redirect" title="City state">city state</a> (an Italian <a href="/wiki/Thalassocracy" title="Thalassocracy">thalassocracy</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Repubbliche_Marinare" class="mw-redirect" title="Repubbliche Marinare">Repubblica Marinara</a></i>: the other three of these were <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amalfi" title="Amalfi">Amalfi</a>). Its strategic position at the head of the Adriatic made Venetian naval and commercial power almost invulnerable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> With the elimination of pirates along the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatian coast</a>, the city became a flourishing trade center between Western Europe and the rest of the world (especially the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>) with a naval power protecting sea routes from Islamic piracy.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> seized a number of places on the eastern shores of the Adriatic before 1200, mostly for commercial reasons, because <a href="/wiki/Pirate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pirate">pirates</a> based there were a menace to trade. The Doge already carried the titles of Duke of <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a> and Duke of <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">Istria</a>. Later mainland possessions, which extended across <a href="/wiki/Lake_Garda" title="Lake Garda">Lake Garda</a> as far west as the <a href="/wiki/Adda_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Adda River">Adda River</a>, were known as the "Terraferma", and were acquired partly as a buffer against belligerent neighbours, partly to guarantee <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alpine</a> trade routes, and partly to ensure the supply of mainland wheat, on which the city depended. In building its maritime commercial empire, the Republic dominated the trade in salt,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> acquired control of most of the islands in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, and became a major power-broker in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. By the standards of the time, Venice's stewardship of its mainland territories was relatively enlightened and the citizens of such towns as <a href="/wiki/Bergamo" title="Bergamo">Bergamo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verona,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Verona, Italy">Verona</a> rallied to the defence of Venetian sovereignty when it was threatened by invaders.</p>
<p>Venice remained closely associated with Constantinople, being twice granted trading privileges in the Eastern Roman Empire, through the so-called <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Bull">Golden Bulls</a> or "chrysobulls" in return for aiding the Eastern Empire to resist Norman and Turkish incursions. In the first chrysobull, Venice acknowledged its homage to the Empire; but not in the second, reflecting the decline of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and the rise of Venice's power.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Venice became an imperial power following the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, which, having veered off course, culminated in 1204 by capturing and sacking <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and establishing the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>. As a result of this conquest, considerable Byzantine plunder was brought back to Venice. This plunder included the <a href="/wiki/Horses_of_Saint_Mark" title="Horses of Saint Mark">gilt bronze horses</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">Hippodrome of Constantinople</a>, which were originally placed above the entrance to the cathedral of Venice, <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Basilica" title="St Mark's Basilica">St Mark's Basilica</a>, although the originals have been replaced with replicas and are now stored within the basilica. After the fall of Constantinople, the former Roman Empire was partitioned among the Latin crusaders and the Venetians. Venice subsequently carved out a sphere of influence in the Mediterranean known as the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_the_Archipelago" title="Duchy of the Archipelago">Duchy of the Archipelago</a>, and captured Crete.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The seizure of Constantinople proved as decisive a factor in ending the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> as the loss of the <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> <a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_administrative_unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theme (Byzantine administrative unit)">themes</a> after <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Manzikert</a>. Although the Byzantines recovered control of the ravaged city a half-century later, the Byzantine Empire was terminally weakened, and existed as a ghost of its old self until <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Mehmet_The_Conqueror" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan Mehmet The Conqueror">Sultan Mehmet The Conqueror</a> took the city <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">in 1453</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Venice09.jpg/220px-Venice09.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Venice09.jpg/330px-Venice09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Venice09.jpg/440px-Venice09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1756" data-file-height="1144" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/San_Giorgio_Maggiore" title="San Giorgio Maggiore">San Giorgio Maggiore Island</a> from <a href="/wiki/St._Mark%27s_Campanile" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Mark's Campanile">St. Mark's Campanile</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Situated on the Adriatic Sea, Venice always traded extensively with the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>. By the late 13th century, Venice was the most prosperous city in all of Europe. At the peak of its power and wealth, it had 36,000 sailors operating 3,300 ships, dominating Mediterranean commerce. Venice's leading families vied with each other to build the grandest palaces and support the work of the greatest and most talented artists. The city was governed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Council_of_Venice" title="Great Council of Venice">Great Council</a>, which was made up of members of the noble families of Venice. The Great Council appointed all public officials and elected a Senate of 200 to 300 individuals. Since this group was too large for efficient administration, a <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ten" title="Council of Ten">Council of Ten</a> (also called the Ducal Council or the Signoria), controlled much of the administration of the city. One member of the great council was elected "<a href="/wiki/Doges_of_Venice" class="mw-redirect" title="Doges of Venice">Doge</a>", or duke, the chief executive, who usually held the title until his death; although several Doges were forced by pressure from their <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchical</a> peers to resign and retire into <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastic</a> seclusion when they were felt to have been discredited by political failure.</p>
<p>The Venetian government structure was similar in some ways to the republican system of ancient Rome, with an elected chief executive (the Doge), a senate-like assembly of nobles, and a mass of citizens with limited political power, who originally had the power to grant or withhold their approval of each newly elected Doge. Church and various private properties were tied to military service, although there was no <a href="/w/index.php?title=Knight_tenure&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Knight tenure (page does not exist)">knight tenure</a> within the city itself. The <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cavalieri_di_San_Marco&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cavalieri di San Marco (page does not exist)">Cavalieri di San Marco</a></i> was the only order of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a> ever instituted in Venice, and no citizen could accept or join a foreign order without the government's consent. Venice remained a republic throughout its independent period, and politics and the military were kept separate, except when on occasion the Doge personally headed the military. War was regarded as a continuation of commerce by other means (hence, the city's early production of large numbers of mercenaries for service elsewhere, and later its reliance on foreign mercenaries when the ruling class was preoccupied with commerce).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Grand_Canal,_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg/220px-The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg/330px-The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg/440px-The_Grand_Canal%2C_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="481" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Grand_Canal,_Venice_c1760_Francesco_Guardi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Francesco_Guardi" title="Francesco Guardi">Francesco Guardi</a>, <i>The Grand Canal</i>, 1760 (<a href="/wiki/Art_Institute_of_Chicago" title="Art Institute of Chicago">Art Institute of Chicago</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg/220px-Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg/330px-Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg/440px-Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2744" data-file-height="1705" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_Guardi%27s_Regatta_in_Venice_at_the_Frick_Art_Reference_Library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph of Guardi's Regatta in Venice at the Frick Art Reference Library.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the people of Venice generally remained orthodox Roman Catholics, the state of Venice was notable for its freedom from religious fanaticism and executed nobody for religious heresy during the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>. This apparent lack of zeal contributed to Venice's frequent conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>. In this context, the writings of the Anglican divine <a href="/wiki/William_Bedell" title="William Bedell">William Bedell</a> are particularly illuminating. Venice was threatened with the <a href="/wiki/Interdict" title="Interdict">interdict</a> on a number of occasions, and twice suffered its imposition. The second, most noted, occasion was in 1606, by order of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a>.</p>
<p>Venetian ambassadors sent home still-extant secret reports of the politics and rumours of European courts, providing fascinating information to modern historians.</p>
<p>The newly invented German <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> spread rapidly throughout Europe in the 15th century, and Venice was quick to adopt it. By 1482, Venice was the printing capital of the world, and the leading printer was <a href="/wiki/Aldus_Manutius" title="Aldus Manutius">Aldus Manutius</a>, who invented paperback books that could be carried in a saddlebag. His <i>Aldine Editions</i> included translations of nearly all the known Greek manuscripts of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">Decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg/220px-Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg/330px-Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg/440px-Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7307" data-file-height="4912" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canal_Grande_Chiesa_della_Salute_e_Dogana_dal_ponte_dell_Accademia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(Venice)" title="Grand Canal (Venice)">Grand Canal</a> in Venice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Venice's long decline started in the 15th century, when it first made an unsuccessful attempt to hold <a href="/wiki/Thessalonica" class="mw-redirect" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a> against the Ottomans (1423–1430). It also sent ships to help defend Constantinople against the besieging Turks (1453). After Constantinople fell to <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Mehmet_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan Mehmet II">Sultan Mehmet II</a>, he declared war on Venice. The war lasted thirty years and cost Venice much of its eastern Mediterranean possessions. Next, Christopher Columbus discovered the New World in 1492. Then Vasco da Gama of Portugal found a sea route to India by rounding the Cape of Good Hope during his first voyage of 1497–99, destroying Venice's land route monopoly. France, England and the Dutch Republic followed. Venice's oared galleys were at a disadvantage when it came to traversing the great oceans, and therefore Venice was left behind in the race for colonies.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> devastated Venice in 1348 and once again between 1575 and 1577.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In three years, the <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> killed some 50,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In 1630, the <a href="/wiki/Italian_plague_of_1629%E2%80%9331" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian plague of 1629–31">Italian plague of 1629–31</a> killed a third of Venice's 150,000 citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Venice began to lose its position as a center of <a href="/wiki/History_of_international_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="History of international trade">international trade</a> during the later part of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> as Portugal became Europe's principal intermediary in the trade with the East, striking at the very foundation of Venice's great wealth; while France and Spain fought for <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> over Italy in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Wars" title="Italian Wars">Italian Wars</a>, marginalising its political influence. However, the Venetian empire was a major exporter of agricultural products, and until the mid-18th century, a significant <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> center.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_age">Modern age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:3008px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg" class="image" title="1870s panoramic view of Venice."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg/3000px-Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg" width="3000" height="214" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg/4500px-Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg/6000px-Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="17952" data-file-height="1280" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_of_Venice_1870s.jpg" title="File:Panorama of Venice 1870s.jpg"></a></div>
1870s panoramic view of Venice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 18th century, Venice became perhaps the most elegant and refined city in Europe, greatly influencing art, architecture and literature. But the Republic lost its independence when <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> conquered Venice on 12 May 1797 during the <a href="/wiki/First_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="First Coalition">First Coalition</a>. Napoleon was seen as something of a liberator by the city's Jewish population, although it can be argued they had lived with fewer restrictions in Venice. He removed the gates of the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Ghetto" title="Venetian Ghetto">Ghetto</a> and ended the restrictions on when and where Jews could live and travel in the city.</p>
<p>Venice became Austrian territory when Napoleon signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Campo_Formio" title="Treaty of Campo Formio">Treaty of Campo Formio</a> on 12 October 1797. The Austrians took control of the city on 18 January 1798. But Venice was taken from Austria by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Pressburg_(1805)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Pressburg (1805)">Treaty of Pressburg</a> in 1805 and became part of Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Napoleonic)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Napoleonic)">Kingdom of Italy</a>; however it was returned to Austria following Napoleon's defeat in 1814, when it became part of the Austrian-held <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lombardy-Venetia" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Lombardy-Venetia">Kingdom of Lombardy-Venetia</a>. In 1848–9, a revolt briefly re-established the <a href="/wiki/Repubblica_di_San_Marco" class="mw-redirect" title="Repubblica di San Marco">Venetian Republic</a> under <a href="/wiki/Daniele_Manin" title="Daniele Manin">Daniele Manin</a>. In 1866, after the <a href="/wiki/Third_Italian_War_of_Independence" title="Third Italian War of Independence">Third Italian War of Independence</a>, Venice, along with the rest of the Veneto, became part of the newly created <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a>.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>, the historic city was largely free from attack, the only aggressive effort of note being <a href="/wiki/Operation_Bowler" title="Operation Bowler">Operation Bowler</a>, a successful <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> precision strike on the German naval operations in the city in March 1945. The targets were destroyed with virtually no architectural damage inflicted on the city itself.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> However the industrial areas in Mestre and Marghera and the railway lines to Padua, Trieste and Trento were <a href="/wiki/Oil_Campaign_chronology_of_World_War_II" title="Oil Campaign chronology of World War II">repeatedly bombed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> On 29 April 1945, <a href="/wiki/2nd_New_Zealand_Division" title="2nd New Zealand Division">New Zealand</a> troops under <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Freyberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard Freyberg">Freyberg</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Eighth Army (United Kingdom)">Eighth Army</a> reached Venice and relieved the city and the mainland, which were already in <a href="/wiki/Italian_resistance_movement" title="Italian resistance movement">partisan</a> hands.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidence">Subsidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Subsidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Acqua_alta" title="Acqua alta">Acqua alta</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg/220px-Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg/330px-Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg/440px-Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1965" data-file-height="1512" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venezia_acqua_alta_notte_2005_modificata.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Acqua_alta" title="Acqua alta">Acqua alta</a>, or high water in Venice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice.longshot.981pix_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg/220px-Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg/330px-Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg/440px-Venice.longshot.981pix_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="981" data-file-height="663" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice.longshot.981pix_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Venice and surroundings in false colour, from <a href="/wiki/Terra_(satellite)" title="Terra (satellite)">Terra</a>. The picture is oriented with North at the top.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">Subsidence</a>, the gradual lowering of the surface of Venice, has led to the seasonal <i><a href="/wiki/Acqua_alta" title="Acqua alta">Acqua alta</a></i> when much of the city's surface is occasionally covered at high tide.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundations">Foundations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Foundations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The buildings of Venice are constructed on closely spaced wooden <a href="/wiki/Deep_foundation" title="Deep foundation">piles</a>. Most of these piles are still intact after centuries of submersion. The foundations rest on plates of <a href="/wiki/Istrian_stone" title="Istrian stone">Istrian limestone</a> placed on top of the piles,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and buildings of brick or <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">stone</a> sit above these footings. The piles penetrate a softer layer of <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mud" title="Mud">mud</a> until they reach a much harder layer of compressed <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a>.</p>
<p>Submerged by water, in oxygen-poor conditions, wood does not <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a> as rapidly as on the surface.</p>
<p>Most of these piles were made from trunks of <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> trees,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> a wood noted for its water resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The alder came from the westernmost part of today's <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> (resulting in the barren land of the <a href="/wiki/Kras" class="mw-redirect" title="Kras">Kras</a> region), in two regions of Croatia, <a href="/wiki/Lika" title="Lika">Lika</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorski_kotar" title="Gorski kotar">Gorski kotar</a> (resulting in the barren slopes of <a href="/wiki/Velebit" title="Velebit">Velebit</a>) and south of <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The city is often threatened by flood <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> pushing in from the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a> between autumn and early spring. Six hundred years ago, Venetians protected themselves from land-based attacks by diverting all the major rivers flowing into the lagoon and thus preventing sediment from filling the area around the city.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This created an ever-deeper lagoon environment.</p>
<p>In 1604, to defray the cost of flood relief, Venice introduced what could be considered the first example of a '<a href="/wiki/Stamp_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamp tax">stamp tax</a>'.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When the revenue fell short of expectations in 1608, Venice introduced paper with the superscription 'AQ' and imprinted instructions, which was to be used for 'letters to officials'. At first, this was to be a temporary tax, but it remained in effect until the fall of the Republic in 1797. Shortly after the introduction of the tax, Spain produced similar paper for general taxation purposes, and the practice spread to other countries.</p>
<p>During the 20th century, when many <a href="/wiki/Artesian_aquifer" title="Artesian aquifer">artesian wells</a> were sunk into the periphery of the lagoon to draw water for local industry, Venice began to <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subside</a>. It was realised that extraction of water from the <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifer</a> was the cause. The sinking has slowed markedly since artesian wells were banned in the 1960s. However, the city is still threatened by more frequent low-level floods (called <i>Acqua alta</i>, "high water") that creep to a height of several centimetres over its quays, regularly following certain tides. In many old houses, the former staircases used to unload goods are now flooded, rendering the former ground floor uninhabitable.</p>
<p>Studies indicate that the city continues sinking at a relatively slow rate of 1-2mm per annum;<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> therefore, the state of alert has not been revoked. In May 2003, the Italian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a> inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/MOSE_project" class="mw-redirect" title="MOSE project">MOSE project</a> (Modulo Sperimentale Elettromeccanico), an experimental model for evaluating the performance of hollow floatable gates; the idea is to fix a series of 78 hollow <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_(boat)" title="Pontoon (boat)">pontoons</a> to the sea bed across the three entrances to the lagoon. When tides are predicted to rise above 110 centimetres, the pontoons will be filled with air, causing them to float and block the incoming water from the Adriatic Sea. This engineering work is due to be completed by 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg/220px-Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg.png" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg/330px-Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg/440px-Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="273" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sestieri_di_Venezia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sestieri of Venice:<br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:purple; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> Cannaregio</span>; <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:blue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> Castello</span>; <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:yellow; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> Dorsoduro</span>; <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:teal; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> San Marco</span>; <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> San Polo</span>; <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> Santa Croce</span>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The whole <i>pensolon</i> (municipality) is divided into 6 boroughs. One of these (the historic city) is divided into six areas called <i><a href="/wiki/Sestiere" title="Sestiere">sestieri</a></i>: <a href="/wiki/Cannaregio" title="Cannaregio">Cannaregio</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Polo" title="San Polo">San Polo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorsoduro" title="Dorsoduro">Dorsoduro</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Giudecca" title="Giudecca">Giudecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isola_Sacca_Fisola" class="mw-redirect" title="Isola Sacca Fisola">Isola Sacca Fisola</a>), <a href="/wiki/Santa_Croce_(Venice)" title="Santa Croce (Venice)">Santa Croce</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Marco_(sestiere_of_Venice)" class="mw-redirect" title="San Marco (sestiere of Venice)">San Marco</a> (including <a href="/wiki/San_Giorgio_Maggiore" title="San Giorgio Maggiore">San Giorgio Maggiore</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Castello,_Venice" title="Castello, Venice">Castello</a> (including <a href="/wiki/San_Pietro_di_Castello_(island)" title="San Pietro di Castello (island)">San Pietro di Castello</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sant%27Elena" title="Sant'Elena">Sant'Elena</a>). Each sestiere was administered by a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/procurator" class="extiw" title="wikt:procurator">procurator</a> and his staff. Nowadays each <i>sestiere</i> is a statistical and historical area without any degree of autonomy. The six fingers or flanges of the <i>ferro</i> on the bow of a <a href="/wiki/Gondola" title="Gondola">gondola</a> represent the six <i>sestieri</i>.</p>
<p>The <i>sestieri</i> are divided into parishes – initially 70 in 1033, but reduced under <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> and now numbering just 38. These parishes predate the <i>sestieri</i>, which were created in about 1170.</p>
<p>Other islands of the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Lagoon" title="Venetian Lagoon">Venetian Lagoon</a> do not form part of any of the <i>sestieri</i>, having historically enjoyed a considerable degree of autonomy.</p>
<p>Each <i>sestiere</i> has its own <a href="/wiki/House_numbering" title="House numbering">house numbering</a> system. Each house has a unique number in the district, from one to several thousand, generally numbered from one corner of the area to another, but not usually in a readily understandable manner.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a>, Venice has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">Humid subtropical climate</a> (<i>Cfa</i>), with cool winters and very warm summers. The 24-hour average in January is 3.3&#160;°C (37.9&#160;°F), and for July this figure is 23.0&#160;°C (73.4&#160;°F). Precipitation is spread relatively evenly throughout the year, and averages 748 millimetres (29.4&#160;in).</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Venice" title="Mayor of Venice">Mayor of Venice</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

<p>The legislative body of the <a href="/wiki/Comune" title="Comune">municipality</a> is the City Council (<i>Consiglio Comunale</i>), which is composed of 45 councillors elected every five years with a proportional system, contextually<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to the mayoral elections. The executive body is the City Committee (<i>Giunta Comunale</i>), composed of 12 <a href="/wiki/Assessor_(Italy)" title="Assessor (Italy)">assessors</a> nominated and presided over by a directly elected <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">Mayor</a>.</p>
<p>Venice was governed by center-left parties from the 1990s until the 2010s, when the mayor started to be elected directly. This is remarkable because <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">Veneto</a> has long been a conservative stronghold, with the coalition between the regionalist <a href="/wiki/Lega_Nord" title="Lega Nord">Lega Nord</a> and the center-right <a href="/wiki/Forza_Italia_(2013)" title="Forza Italia (2013)">Forza Italia</a> winning absolute majorities of the electorate in many elections at communal, national, and regional levels.</p>
<p>After a corruption scandal that forced the center-left mayor <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Orsoni" title="Giorgio Orsoni">Giorgio Orsoni</a> to resign, Venice voted for the first time in June 2015 for a conservative directly-elected mayor: the center-right businessman <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Brugnaro" title="Luigi Brugnaro">Luigi Brugnaro</a> won the election in the second round of voting with the 53% of the votes against the leftist magistrate and member of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian Senate">Italian Senate</a> <a href="/wiki/Felice_Casson" title="Felice Casson">Felice Casson</a>, who led in the first round with 38% of the votes.</p>
<p>The municipality of Venice is subdivided into six administrative Boroughs (<i>Municipalità</i>). Each Borough is governed by a Council (<i>Consiglio</i>) and a President, elected contextually<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to the city Mayor. The urban organization is governed by the Italian Constitution (art. 114). The Boroughs have the power to advise the Mayor with nonbinding opinions on a large spectrum of topics (environment, construction, public health, local markets) and exercise the functions delegated to them by the City Council; in addition they are supplied with autonomous funding to finance local activities. The Boroughs are:</p>
<p><i>Lagoon area:</i></p>
<ul>
<li>Venezia (<i>historic city</i>)-<a href="/wiki/Murano" title="Murano">Murano</a>–<a href="/wiki/Burano" title="Burano">Burano</a> (also known as <i>Venezia insulare</i>); population: 69,136;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lido_di_Venezia" title="Lido di Venezia">Lido</a>–<a href="/wiki/Pellestrina" title="Pellestrina">Pellestrina</a> (also known as <i>Venezia litorale</i>); population: 21,664.</li>
</ul>
<p><i>Mainland (terraferma)</i>, annexed with a Royal Decree, in 1926, to the municipality of Venezia:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Favaro_Veneto" title="Favaro Veneto">Favaro Veneto</a>; population: 23,615;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mestre" title="Mestre">Mestre</a>-Carpenedo (also known as <i>Mestre centro</i>); population: 88,952;</li>
<li>Chirignago-Zelarino; population: 38;179;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marghera" title="Marghera">Marghera</a>; population: 28;466.</li>
</ul>
<p>After the 2015 elections, five of the six boroughs are governed by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(Italy)" title="Democratic Party (Italy)">Democratic Party</a> and its allies, and one by the center-right mayoral majority.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Venice" title="Economic history of Venice">Venice's economy</a> has changed throughout history. Although there is little specific information about the earliest years, it is likely that an important source of the city's prosperity was the trade in slaves, captured in central Europe and sold to North Africa and the Levant. Venice's location at the head of the Adriatic, and directly south of the terminus of the Brenner Pass over the Alps, would have given it a distinct advantage as a middleman in this important trade. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, Venice was a major center for commerce and trade, as it controlled a vast sea-empire, and became an extremely wealthy European city, a leader in political and economic affairs and a centre for trade and commerce.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutvenice.org_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutvenice.org-40">[40]</a></sup> From the 11th century until the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimages</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> were offered in Venice. Other ports such as <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancona" title="Ancona">Ancona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">Dubrovnik</a> were hardly able to make any competition to the well organized transportation of pilgrims from Venice.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This all changed by the 17th century, when Venice's trade empire was taken over by other countries such as Portugal, and its naval importance was reduced. In the 18th century, then, it became a major agricultural and industrial exporter. The 18th century's biggest industrial complex was the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Arsenal" class="mw-redirect" title="Venice Arsenal">Venice Arsenal</a>, and the Italian Army still uses it today (even though some space has been used for major theatrical and cultural productions, and spaces for art).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Today, Venice's economy is mainly based on tourism, shipbuilding (mainly done in the neighboring cities of <a href="/wiki/Mestre" title="Mestre">Mestre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porto_Marghera" class="mw-redirect" title="Porto Marghera">Porto Marghera</a>), services, trade and industrial exports.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutvenice.org_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutvenice.org-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Murano_glass" title="Murano glass">Murano glass</a> production in <a href="/wiki/Murano" title="Murano">Murano</a> and lace production in <a href="/wiki/Burano" title="Burano">Burano</a> are also highly important to the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutvenice.org_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutvenice.org-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice_IMG_3731.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Venice_IMG_3731.JPG/220px-Venice_IMG_3731.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Venice_IMG_3731.JPG/330px-Venice_IMG_3731.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Venice_IMG_3731.JPG/440px-Venice_IMG_3731.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice_IMG_3731.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Piazza San Marco. Doge's Palace.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Venice is one of the most important tourist destinations in the world for its celebrated art and architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">[44]</a></sup> The city has an average of 50,000 tourists a day (2007 estimate).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In 2006, it was the world's 28th most internationally visited city, with 2.927&#160;million international arrivals that year.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venise_et_le_Lido,_travel_poster_for_ENIT,_ca._1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg/170px-Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg/255px-Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg/340px-Venise_et_le_Lido%2C_travel_poster_for_ENIT%2C_ca._1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3284" data-file-height="5170" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venise_et_le_Lido,_travel_poster_for_ENIT,_ca._1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Travel poster from c. 1920 for Venice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tourism has been a major sector of Venetian industry since the 18th century, when it was a major center for the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Tour" title="Grand Tour">Grand Tour</a>, with its beautiful cityscape, uniqueness, and rich musical and artistic cultural heritage. In the 19th century, it became a fashionable centre for the "rich and famous", often staying or dining at luxury establishments such as the Danieli Hotel and the <a href="/wiki/Caff%C3%A8_Florian" title="Caffè Florian">Caffè Florian</a>. It continued being a fashionable city in vogue right into the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">[44]</a></sup> In the 1980s, the <a href="/wiki/Carnival_of_Venice" title="Carnival of Venice">Carnival of Venice</a> was revived and the city has become a major centre of international conferences and festivals, such as the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Venice_Biennale" title="Venice Biennale">Venice Biennale</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Film_Festival" title="Venice Film Festival">Venice Film Festival</a>, which attract visitors from all over the world for their theatrical, cultural, cinematic, artistic, and musical productions<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, there are numerous attractions in Venice, such as <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Basilica" title="St Mark's Basilica">St Mark's Basilica</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(Venice)" title="Grand Canal (Venice)">Grand Canal</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Piazza_San_Marco" title="Piazza San Marco">Piazza San Marco</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Lido_di_Venezia" title="Lido di Venezia">Lido di Venezia</a> is also a popular international luxury destination, attracting thousands of actors, critics, celebrities, and mainly people in the cinematic industry. The city also relies heavily on the cruise business.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">[44]</a></sup> The Cruise Venice Committee has estimated that cruise ship passengers spend more than 150 million euros (US $193 million) annually in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg/220px-Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg/330px-Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg/440px-Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6504" data-file-height="4344" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cruiseship_passing_bacino_San_Marco_Venise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cruiseship passing bacino San Marco Venise</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, Venice's popularity as a major worldwide tourist destination has caused several problems, including the fact that the city can be very overcrowded at some points of the year. It is regarded by some as a tourist trap, and by others as a "living museum".<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">[44]</a></sup> Unlike most other places in Western Europe, and the world, Venice has become widely known for its element of <a href="/wiki/Elegant_decay" title="Elegant decay">elegant decay</a>. The competition for foreigners to buy homes in Venice has made prices rise so high that numerous inhabitants are forced to move to more affordable areas of <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">Veneto</a> and Italy, the most notable being <a href="/wiki/Mestre" title="Mestre">Mestre</a>.</p>
<p>The need to balance cruise tourism revenues with the protection of the city's fragile canals has seen the Italian Transport Ministry attempt to introduce a ban on large cruise ships visiting the city. The ban would only allow cruise ships smaller than 40,000-gross tons to enter Venice’s Giudecca Canal and St Mark's basin.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In January, a regional court scrapped the ban, but global cruise lines indicated that they would continue to respect it until a long-term solution for the protection of Venice is found.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The city considered a ban on <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheeled</a> <a href="/wiki/Suitcase" title="Suitcase">suitcases</a>, but settled on banning hard wheels for cargo from May 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_historical_city">In the historical city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Venice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In the historical city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg/220px-Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg/330px-Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg/440px-Venice_as_seen_from_the_air_with_bridge_to_mainland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg/220px-Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg/330px-Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg/440px-Giudecca_IMG_3817.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3996" data-file-height="2140" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
